   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Ray Kurzweil
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">

     <p>
      <b>
       Raymond "Ray" Kurzweil
      </b>
      (
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="IPA nopopups">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">
         /
         <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
          <span title="// primary stress follows">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
           k
          </span>
          <span title="/r/ 'ir' in 'bird'">
           r
          </span>
          <span title="'z' in 'Zion'">
           z
          </span>
          <span title="'w' in 'wind'">
           w
          </span>
          <span title="/a/ long 'i' in 'tide'">
           a
          </span>
          <span title="'l' in 'lie'">
           l
          </span>
         </span>
         /
        </a>
       </span>
      </span>
      <span class="Unicode" title="English pronunciation respelling">
       <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Wikipedia:Pronunciation respelling key">
        <i>
         <b>
          <span class="smallcaps">
           <span class="SMALLCAPS" style="FONT-VARIANT:SMALL-CAPS;">
            <span class="NOCAPS" style="TEXT-TRANSFORM:LOWERCASE;">
             KURZ
            </span>
           </span>
          </span>
         </b>
         -wyl
        </i>
       </a>
      </span>
      ; born February 12, 1948) is an American author, computer scientist, inventor,
      <a href="/wiki/Futurist" title="Futurist">
       futurist
      </a>
      , and is a director of engineering at
      <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">
       Google
      </a>
      . Aside from futurology, he is involved in fields such as
      <a href="/wiki/Optical_character_recognition" title="Optical character recognition">
       optical character recognition
      </a>
      (OCR),
      <a href="/wiki/Speech_synthesis" title="Speech synthesis">
       text-to-speech synthesis
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Speech_recognition" title="Speech recognition">
       speech recognition
      </a>
      technology, and electronic keyboard instruments. He has written books on health,
      <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">
       artificial intelligence
      </a>
      (AI),
      <a href="/wiki/Transhumanism" title="Transhumanism">
       transhumanism
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Technological_singularity" title="Technological singularity">
       technological singularity
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Futures_studies" title="Futures studies">
       futurism
      </a>
      . Kurzweil is a public advocate for the futurist and transhumanist movements, as has been displayed in his vast collection of public talks, wherein he has shared his primarily optimistic outlooks on
      <a href="/wiki/Life_extension" title="Life extension">
       life extension
      </a>
      technologies and the future of nanotechnology, robotics, and biotechnology.
     </p>
     <p>
      Kurzweil was the principal inventor of the first
      <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">
       charge-coupled device
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flatbed_scanner" title="Flatbed scanner">
       flatbed scanner
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inventnow_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-inventnow-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the first omni-font
      <a href="/wiki/Optical_character_recognition" title="Optical character recognition">
       optical character recognition
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inventnow_2-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-inventnow-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the first
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Print-to-speech" title="Print-to-speech">
       print-to-speech
      </a>
      reading machine for the blind,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the first commercial text-to-speech synthesizer,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the
      <a href="/wiki/Kurzweil_K250" title="Kurzweil K250">
       Kurzweil K250
      </a>
      music synthesizer capable of simulating the sound of the grand piano and other orchestral instruments, and the first commercially marketed large-vocabulary speech recognition.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KurzweilAIbio_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-KurzweilAIbio-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Kurzweil received the 1999
      <a href="/wiki/National_Medal_of_Technology_and_Innovation" title="National Medal of Technology and Innovation">
       National Medal of Technology and Innovation
      </a>
      , the United States' highest honor in technology, from President Clinton in a White House ceremony. He was the recipient of the $500,000
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lemelson-MIT_Prize" title="Lemelson-MIT Prize">
       Lemelson-MIT Prize
      </a>
      for 2001,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the world's largest for innovation. And in 2002 he was inducted into the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Inventors_Hall_of_Fame" title="National Inventors Hall of Fame">
       National Inventors Hall of Fame
      </a>
      , established by the U.S. Patent Office. He has received twenty honorary doctorates, and honors from three U.S. presidents. Kurzweil has been described as a "restless genius"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bulkeley_A3A_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Bulkeley_A3A-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      by
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">
        The Wall Street Journal
       </a>
      </i>
      and "the ultimate thinking machine"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pfeiffer_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pfeiffer-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      by
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">
        Forbes
       </a>
      </i>
      . PBS included Kurzweil as one of 16 "revolutionaries who made America"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Who_Made_America_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Who_Made_America-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      along with other inventors of the past two centuries.
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Inc._(magazine)" title="Inc. (magazine)">
        Inc.
       </a>
      </i>
      magazine ranked him #8 among the "most fascinating" entrepreneurs in the United States and called him "Edison's rightful heir".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26_Most_Fascinating_Entrepreneurs_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-26_Most_Fascinating_Entrepreneurs-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Kurzweil has authored seven books, five of which have been national bestsellers.
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Age_of_Spiritual_Machines" title="The Age of Spiritual Machines">
        The Age of Spiritual Machines
       </a>
      </i>
      has been translated into 9 languages and was the #1 best-selling book on
      <a href="/wiki/Amazon.com" title="Amazon.com">
       Amazon
      </a>
      in science. Kurzweil's book
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Singularity_Is_Near" title="The Singularity Is Near">
        The Singularity Is Near
       </a>
      </i>
      was a
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Times" title="New York Times">
        New York Times
       </a>
      </i>
      bestseller, and has been the #1 book on Amazon in both science and philosophy. His latest bestseller is
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/How_to_Create_a_Mind" title="How to Create a Mind">
        How to Create a Mind: The Secret of Human Thought Revealed
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Kurzweil speaks widely to audiences public and private and regularly delivers keynote speeches at
      <a href="/wiki/Economic_sector" title="Economic sector">
       industry
      </a>
      conferences like
      <a href="/wiki/DEMO_conference" title="DEMO conference">
       DEMO
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SXSW" title="SXSW">
       SXSW
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/TED_(conference)" title="TED (conference)">
       TED
      </a>
      . His website catalogs his public speaking, publications and media appearances.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He maintains the news website KurzweilAI.net, which has over three million readers annually.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KurzweilAIbio_5-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-KurzweilAIbio-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

         <span class="toctext">
          Movies
         </span>
        </a>
       </li>
       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9">

     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Life.2C_inventions.2C_and_business_career">
       Life, inventions, and business career
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ray_Kurzweil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Life, inventions, and business career">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">
       Early life
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ray_Kurzweil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early life">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Ray Kurzweil grew up in the New York City borough of
      <a href="/wiki/Queens" title="Queens">
       Queens
      </a>
      . He was born to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secular_Jew" title="Secular Jew">
       secular Jewish
      </a>
      parents who had emigrated from
      <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">
       Austria
      </a>
      just before the onset of World War II. He was exposed via
      <a href="/wiki/Unitarian_Universalism" title="Unitarian Universalism">
       Unitarian Universalism
      </a>
      to a diversity of religious faiths during his upbringing. His unitarian church had the philosophy of many paths to the truth  the religious education consisted of spending six months on a single religion before moving onto the next. Kurzweil is an
      <a href="/wiki/Agnosticism" title="Agnosticism">
       agnostic
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      His father was a musician, a noted conductor, and a music educator. His mother was a
      <a href="/wiki/Visual_arts" title="Visual arts">
       visual artist
      </a>
      . Kurzweil decided he wanted to be an inventor at the age of five.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In his youth, Kurzweil was an avid reader of science fiction literature. At the age of eight, nine, and ten, he read the entire
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tom_Swift_Jr" title="Tom Swift Jr">
       Tom Swift Jr
      </a>
      . series. At the age of seven or eight, he built a robotic puppet theater and robotic game. He was involved with computers and built computing devices by the age of twelve. At the age of fourteen, Kurzweil wrote a paper detailing his theory of the
      <a href="/wiki/Neocortex" title="Neocortex">
       neocortex
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      His parents were involved with the arts, and he is quoted in the documentary
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Transcendent_Man" title="Transcendent Man">
        Transcendent Man
       </a>
      </i>
      as saying that the household always produced discussions about the future and technology.
     </p>
     <p>
      Kurzweil attended
      <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren_High_School" title="Martin Van Buren High School">
       Martin Van Buren High School
      </a>
      . During class, he often held onto his class textbooks to seemingly participate, but instead, focused on his own projects which were hidden behind the book. His uncle, an engineer at
      <a href="/wiki/Bell_Labs" title="Bell Labs">
       Bell Labs
      </a>
      , taught young Kurzweil the basics of
      <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">
       computer science
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1963, at age fifteen, he wrote his first computer program.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He created a pattern-recognition software program that analyzed the works of classical composers, and then synthesized its own songs in similar styles. In 1965, he was invited to appear on the
      <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">
       CBS
      </a>
      television program
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/I%27ve_Got_a_Secret" title="I've Got a Secret">
        I've Got a Secret
       </a>
      </i>
      , where he performed a piano piece that was composed by a computer he also had built.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-booktv_18-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-booktv-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Later that year, he won first prize in the International Science Fair for the invention;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ssp_19-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ssp-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Kurzweil's submission to
      <a href="/wiki/Intel_Science_Talent_Search" title="Intel Science Talent Search">
       Westinghouse Talent Search
      </a>
      of his first computer program alongside several other projects resulted in him being one of its national winners, which allowed him to be personally congratulated by President
      <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">
       Lyndon B. Johnson
      </a>
      during a
      <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">
       White House
      </a>
      ceremony. These activities collectively impressed upon Kurzweil the belief that nearly any problem could be overcome.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Mid-life">
       Mid-life
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ray_Kurzweil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Mid-life">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      While in high school, Kurzweil had corresponded with
      <a href="/wiki/Marvin_Minsky" title="Marvin Minsky">
       Marvin Minsky
      </a>
      and was invited to visit him at MIT, which he did. Kurzweil also visited Professor Rosenblatt at Cornell.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      He obtained a B.S. in computer science and literature in 1970 at
      <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">
       MIT
      </a>
      . He went to MIT to study with
      <a href="/wiki/Marvin_Minsky" title="Marvin Minsky">
       Marvin Minsky
      </a>
      . He took all of the computer programming courses (eight or nine) offered at MIT in the first year and a half.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1968, during his sophomore year at
      <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">
       MIT
      </a>
      , Kurzweil started a company that used a computer program to match high school students with colleges. The program, called the Select College Consulting Program, was designed by him and compared thousands of different criteria about each college with questionnaire answers submitted by each student applicant. Around this time, he sold the company to Harcourt, Brace &amp; World for $100,000 (roughly $670,000 in 2013 dollars) plus royalties.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1974, Kurzweil founded Kurzweil Computer Products, Inc. and led development of the first omni-font
      <a href="/wiki/Optical_character_recognition" title="Optical character recognition">
       optical character recognition
      </a>
      system, a computer program capable of recognizing text written in any normal font. Before that time, scanners had only been able to read text written in a few fonts. He decided that the best application of this technology would be to create a reading machine, which would allow blind people to understand written text by having a computer read it to them aloud. However, this device required the invention of two enabling technologiesthe
      <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">
       CCD
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flatbed_scanner" title="Flatbed scanner">
       flatbed scanner
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Speech_synthesis" title="Speech synthesis">
       text-to-speech
      </a>
      synthesizer. Development of these technologies was completed at other institutions such as Bell Labs, and on January 13, 1976, the finished product was unveiled during a news conference headed by him and the leaders of the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Federation_of_the_Blind" title="National Federation of the Blind">
       National Federation of the Blind
      </a>
      . Called the Kurzweil Reading Machine, the device covered an entire tabletop.
     </p>
     <p>
      Kurzweil's next major business venture began in 1978, when Kurzweil Computer Products began selling a commercial version of the optical character recognition computer program.
      <a href="/wiki/LexisNexis" title="LexisNexis">
       LexisNexis
      </a>
      was one of the first customers, and bought the program to upload paper legal and news documents onto its nascent online databases.
     </p>
     <p>
      Kurzweil sold his company to Lernout &amp; Hauspie. Following the bankruptcy of the latter, the system became a subsidiary of Xerox formerly known as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scansoft" title="Scansoft">
       Scansoft
      </a>
      and now as
      <a href="/wiki/Nuance_Communications" title="Nuance Communications">
       Nuance Communications
      </a>
      , and he functioned as a consultant for the former until 1995.
     </p>
     <p>
      Kurzweil's next business venture was in the realm of electronic music technology. After a 1982 meeting with
      <a href="/wiki/Stevie_Wonder" title="Stevie Wonder">
       Stevie Wonder
      </a>
      , in which the latter lamented the divide in capabilities and qualities between electronic synthesizers and traditional musical instruments, Kurzweil was inspired to create a new generation of music synthesizers capable of accurately duplicating the sounds of real instruments.
      <a href="/wiki/Kurzweil_Music_Systems" title="Kurzweil Music Systems">
       Kurzweil Music Systems
      </a>
      was founded in the same year, and in 1984, the
      <a href="/wiki/Kurzweil_K250" title="Kurzweil K250">
       Kurzweil K250
      </a>
      was unveiled. The machine was capable of imitating a number of instruments, and in tests musicians were unable to discern the difference between the
      <a href="/wiki/Kurzweil_K250" title="Kurzweil K250">
       Kurzweil K250
      </a>
      on piano mode from a normal grand piano.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The recording and mixing abilities of the machine, coupled with its abilities to imitate different instruments, made it possible for a single user to compose and play an entire orchestral piece.
     </p>
     <p>
      Kurzweil Music Systems was sold to
      <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">
       Korean
      </a>
      musical instrument manufacturer
      <a href="/wiki/Young_Chang" title="Young Chang">
       Young Chang
      </a>
      in 1990. As with
      <a href="/wiki/Xerox" title="Xerox">
       Xerox
      </a>
      , Kurzweil remained as a consultant for several years.
      <a href="/wiki/Hyundai" title="Hyundai">
       Hyundai
      </a>
      acquired Young Chang in 2006 and in January 2007 appointed Raymond Kurzweil as Chief Strategy Officer of Kurzweil Music Systems.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Later_life">
       Later life
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ray_Kurzweil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Later life">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Concurrent with Kurzweil Music Systems, Kurzweil created the company Kurzweil Applied Intelligence (KAI) to develop computer
      <a href="/wiki/Speech_recognition" title="Speech recognition">
       speech recognition
      </a>
      systems for commercial use. The first product, which debuted in 1987, was an early
      <a href="/wiki/Speech_recognition" title="Speech recognition">
       speech recognition
      </a>
      program.
     </p>
     <p>
      Kurzweil started
      <a href="/wiki/Kurzweil_Educational_Systems" title="Kurzweil Educational Systems">
       Kurzweil Educational Systems
      </a>
      in 1996 to develop new pattern-recognition-based computer technologies to help people with disabilities such as blindness,
      <a href="/wiki/Dyslexia" title="Dyslexia">
       dyslexia
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attention-deficit_hyperactivity_disorder" title="Attention-deficit hyperactivity disorder">
       ADD
      </a>
      in school. Products include the Kurzweil 1000 text-to-speech converter software program, which enables a computer to read electronic and scanned text aloud to blind or visually impaired users, and the Kurzweil 3000 program, which is a multifaceted electronic learning system that helps with reading, writing, and
      <a href="/wiki/Study_skills" title="Study skills">
       study skills
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Raymond_Kurzweil,_Stanford_2006_(square_crop).jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1864" data-file-width="1864" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Raymond_Kurzweil%2C_Stanford_2006_%28square_crop%29.jpg/250px-Raymond_Kurzweil%2C_Stanford_2006_%28square_crop%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Raymond_Kurzweil%2C_Stanford_2006_%28square_crop%29.jpg/375px-Raymond_Kurzweil%2C_Stanford_2006_%28square_crop%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Raymond_Kurzweil%2C_Stanford_2006_%28square_crop%29.jpg/500px-Raymond_Kurzweil%2C_Stanford_2006_%28square_crop%29.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Raymond_Kurzweil,_Stanford_2006_(square_crop).jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Raymond Kurzweil at the
        <a href="/wiki/Singularity_Summit" title="Singularity Summit">
         Singularity Summit
        </a>
        at Stanford in 2006
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      During the 1990s, Kurzweil founded the Medical Learning Company.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The company's products included an interactive computer education program for doctors and a computer-simulated patient. Around the same time, Kurzweil started KurzweilCyberArt.coma website featuring computer programs to assist the creative art process. The site used to offer free downloads of a program called AARONa visual art synthesizer developed by Harold Cohenand of "Kurzweil's Cybernetic Poet", which automatically creates poetry. During this period he also started KurzweilAI.net, a website devoted towards showcasing news of scientific developments, publicizing the ideas of high-tech thinkers and critics alike, and promoting futurist-related discussion among the general population through the Mind-X forum.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1999, Kurzweil created a
      <a href="/wiki/Hedge_fund" title="Hedge fund">
       hedge fund
      </a>
      called "FatKat" (Financial Accelerating Transactions from Kurzweil Adaptive Technologies), which began trading in 2006. He has stated that the ultimate aim is to improve the performance of FatKat's A.I. investment software program, enhancing its ability to recognize patterns in "currency fluctuations and stock-ownership trends."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He predicted in his 1999 book,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Age_of_Spiritual_Machines" title="The Age of Spiritual Machines">
        The Age of Spiritual Machines
       </a>
       ,
      </i>
      that computers will one day prove superior to the best human financial minds at making profitable investment decisions. In June 2005, Kurzweil introduced the
      <a href="/wiki/K-NFB_Reader" title="K-NFB Reader">
       "Kurzweil-National Federation of the Blind Reader" (K-NFB Reader)
      </a>
      a pocket-sized device consisting of a digital camera and computer unit. Like the Kurzweil Reading Machine of almost 30 years before, the
      <a href="/wiki/K-NFB_Reader" title="K-NFB Reader">
       K-NFB Reader
      </a>
      is designed to aid blind people by reading written text aloud. The newer machine is portable and scans text through digital camera images, while the older machine is large and scans text through flatbed scanning.
     </p>
     <p>
      In December 2012, Kurzweil was hired by Google in a full-time position to "work on new projects involving machine learning and language processing".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Google co-founder
      <a href="/wiki/Larry_Page" title="Larry Page">
       Larry Page
      </a>
      and Kurzweil agreed on a one-sentence job description: "to bring natural language understanding to Google".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wsj_28-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-wsj-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      He received a Technical Grammy on February 8, 2015, recognizing his diverse technical and creative accomplishments. For purposes of the Grammy, perhaps most notable was the aforementioned Kurzweil K250.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">
       Personal life
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ray_Kurzweil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Personal life">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Kurzweil married Sonya Rosenwald Fenster in 1975 and has two children.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Sonya Kurzweil is a psychologist in private practice and clinical instructor in Psychology at Harvard Medical School; she is interested in the way that digital media can be integrated into the lives of children and teens.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      He has a son, Ethan Kurzweil, a venture capitalist,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and a daughter, Amy Kurzweil.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Creative_approach">
       Creative approach
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ray_Kurzweil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Creative approach">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Kurzweil said "I realize that most inventions fail not because the R&amp;D department cant get them to work, but because the timing is wrong - not all of the enabling factors are at play where they are needed. Inventing is a lot like surfing: you have to anticipate and catch the wave at just the right moment."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      For the past several decades, Kurzweil's most effective and common approach to doing creative work has been conducted during his lucid dreamlike state which immediately precedes his awakening state. He claims to have constructed inventions, solved difficult problems, such as algorithmic, business strategy, organizational, and interpersonal problems, and written speeches in this state.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_21-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Books">
       Books
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ray_Kurzweil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Books">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Kurzweil's first book,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Age_of_Intelligent_Machines" title="The Age of Intelligent Machines">
        The Age of Intelligent Machines
       </a>
      </i>
      , was published in 1990. The nonfiction work discusses the history of computer artificial intelligence (AI) and forecasts future developments. Other experts in the field of AI contribute heavily to the work in the form of essays. The
      <a href="/wiki/Association_of_American_Publishers" title="Association of American Publishers">
       Association of American Publishers
      </a>
      ' awarded it the status of
      <i>
       Most Outstanding Computer Science Book
      </i>
      of 1990.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1993, Kurzweil published a book on nutrition called
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_10%25_Solution_for_a_Healthy_Life" title="The 10% Solution for a Healthy Life">
        The 10% Solution for a Healthy Life
       </a>
      </i>
      . The book's main idea is that high levels of fat intake are the cause of many health disorders common in the U.S., and thus that cutting fat consumption down to 10% of the total calories consumed would be optimal for most people.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1999, Kurzweil published
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Age_of_Spiritual_Machines" title="The Age of Spiritual Machines">
        The Age of Spiritual Machines
       </a>
      </i>
      , which further elucidates his theories regarding the future of technology, which themselves stem from his analysis of long-term trends in biological and technological evolution. Much emphasis is on the likely course of AI development, along with the future of computer architecture.
     </p>
     <p>
      Kurzweil's next book, published in 2004, returned to human health and nutrition.
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Fantastic_Voyage:_Live_Long_Enough_to_Live_Forever" title="Fantastic Voyage: Live Long Enough to Live Forever">
        Fantastic Voyage: Live Long Enough to Live Forever
       </a>
      </i>
      was co-authored by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terry_Grossman" title="Terry Grossman">
       Terry Grossman
      </a>
      , a medical doctor and specialist in alternative medicine.
     </p>
     <p>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Singularity_Is_Near" title="The Singularity Is Near">
        The Singularity Is Near
       </a>
      </i>
      , published in 2005, was made into a movie starring
      <a href="/wiki/Pauley_Perrette" title="Pauley Perrette">
       Pauley Perrette
      </a>
      from
      <a href="/wiki/NCIS_(TV_series)" title="NCIS (TV series)">
       NCIS
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In February 2007, Ptolemaic Productions acquired the rights to
      <i>
       The Singularity is Near
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       The Age of Spiritual Machines
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Fantastic Voyage
      </i>
      including the rights to film Kurzweil's life and ideas for the documentary film
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Transcendent_Man" title="Transcendent Man">
        Transcendent Man
       </a>
      </i>
      , which was directed by Barry Ptolemy.
     </p>
     <p>
      <i>
       Transcend: Nine Steps to Living Well Forever
      </i>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      a follow-up to
      <i>
       Fantastic Voyage
      </i>
      , was released on April 28, 2009.
     </p>
     <p>
      Kurzweil's latest book,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/How_to_Create_a_Mind" title="How to Create a Mind">
        How to Create a Mind: The Secret of Human Thought Revealed
       </a>
       ,
      </i>
      was released on Nov. 13, 2012.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In it Kurzweil describes his Pattern Recognition Theory of Mind, the theory that the
      <a href="/wiki/Neocortex" title="Neocortex">
       neocortex
      </a>
      is a hierarchical system of pattern recognizers, and argues that duplicating this architecture in machines could lead to an artificial superintelligence.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Kurzweil is also writing a novel called
      <i>
       Danielle
      </i>
      , about his imaginary superheroine daughter who solves problems through intelligence.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Movies">
       Movies
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ray_Kurzweil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Movies">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Kurzweil wrote and co-produced a movie directed by Anthony Waller, called
      <i>
       The Singularity Is Near: A True Story About the Future
      </i>
      , in 2010 based, in part, on his 2005 book
      <i>
       The Singularity Is Near
      </i>
      . Part fiction, part non-fiction, he interviews 20 big thinkers like
      <a href="/wiki/Marvin_Minsky" title="Marvin Minsky">
       Marvin Minsky
      </a>
      , plus there is a B-line narrative story that illustrates some of the ideas, where a computer avatar (Ramona) saves the world from self-replicating microscopic robots. In addition to his movie, an independent, feature-length documentary was made about Kurzweil, his life, and his ideas, called
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Transcendent_Man" title="Transcendent Man">
        Transcendent Man
       </a>
      </i>
      . Filmmakers
      <a href="/wiki/Barry_Ptolemy" title="Barry Ptolemy">
       Barry Ptolemy
      </a>
      and Felicia Ptolemy followed Kurzweil, documenting his global speaking-tour. Premiered in 2009 at the
      <a href="/wiki/Tribeca_Film_Festival" title="Tribeca Film Festival">
       Tribeca Film Festival
      </a>
      ,
      <i>
       Transcendent Man
      </i>
      documents Kurzweil's quest to reveal mankind's ultimate destiny and explores many of the ideas found in his New York Times bestselling book,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Singularity_Is_Near" title="The Singularity Is Near">
        The Singularity Is Near
       </a>
      </i>
      , including his concept exponential growth, radical life expansion, and how we will transcend our biology. The Ptolemys documented Kurzweil's stated goal of bringing back his late father using AI. The film also features critics who argue against Kurzweil's predictions.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2010, an independent documentary film called
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Plug_%26_Pray" title="Plug &amp; Pray">
        Plug &amp; Pray
       </a>
      </i>
      premiered at the
      <a href="/wiki/Seattle_International_Film_Festival" title="Seattle International Film Festival">
       Seattle International Film Festival
      </a>
      , in which Kurzweil and one of his major critics, the late
      <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Weizenbaum" title="Joseph Weizenbaum">
       Joseph Weizenbaum
      </a>
      , argue about the benefits of eternal life.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The feature-length documentary film
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Singularity_(film)" title="The Singularity (film)">
        The Singularity
       </a>
      </i>
      by independent filmmaker Doug Wolens (released at the end of 2012), showcasing Kurzweil, has been acclaimed as "a large-scale achievement in its documentation of futurist and counter-futurist ideas and the best documentary on the Singularity to date."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ieet_43-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ieet-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Kurzweil frequently comments on the application of cell-size nanotechnology to the workings of the human brain and how this could be applied to building AI. While being interviewed for a February 2009 issue of
      <i>
       Rolling Stone
      </i>
      magazine, Kurzweil expressed a desire to construct a genetic copy of his late father, Fredric Kurzweil, from DNA within his grave site. This feat would be achieved by exhumation and extraction of DNA, constructing a clone of Fredric and retrieving memories and recollectionsfrom Ray's mindof his father.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Transcendent_Man_44-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Transcendent_Man-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Kurzweil kept all of his father's records, notes, and pictures in order to maintain as much of his father as he could. Ray is known for taking over 200 pills a day, meant to reprogram his biochemistry. This, according to Ray, is only a precursor to the devices at the nano scale that will eventually replace a blood-cell, self updating of specific pathogens to improve the immune system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Transcendent_Man_44-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Transcendent_Man-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Views">
       Views
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ray_Kurzweil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Views">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="The_Law_of_Accelerating_Returns">
       The Law of Accelerating Returns
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ray_Kurzweil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: The Law of Accelerating Returns">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Accelerating_change" title="Accelerating change">
       Accelerating change
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      In his 1999 book
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Age_of_Spiritual_Machines" title="The Age of Spiritual Machines">
        The Age of Spiritual Machines
       </a>
      </i>
      Kurzweil proposed "The Law of Accelerating Returns", according to which the rate of change in a wide variety of evolutionary systems (including the growth of technologies) tends to increase exponentially.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He gave further focus to this issue in a 2001 essay entitled "The Law of Accelerating Returns", which proposed an extension of
      <a href="/wiki/Moore%27s_law" title="Moore's law">
       Moore's law
      </a>
      to a wide variety of technologies, and used this to argue in favor of
      <a href="/wiki/Vernor_Vinge" title="Vernor Vinge">
       Vernor Vinge
      </a>
      's concept of a
      <a href="/wiki/Technological_singularity" title="Technological singularity">
       technological singularity
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Kurzweil suggests that this exponential technological growth is counter-intuitive to the way our brains perceive the worldsince our brains were biologically inherited from humans living in a world that was linear and localand, as a consequence, he claims it has encouraged great skepticism in his future projections.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Stance_on_the_future_of_genetics.2C_nanotechnology.2C_and_robotics">
       Stance on the future of genetics, nanotechnology, and robotics
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ray_Kurzweil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Stance on the future of genetics, nanotechnology, and robotics">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Kurzweil is working with the
      <a href="/wiki/Army_Science_Board" title="Army Science Board">
       Army Science Board
      </a>
      to develop a rapid response system to deal with the possible abuse of biotechnology. He suggests that the same technologies that are empowering us to reprogram biology away from cancer and heart disease could be used by a bioterrorist to reprogram a biological virus to be more deadly, communicable, and stealthy. However, he suggests that we have the scientific tools to successfully defend against these attacks, similar to the way we defend against computer software viruses. He has testified before Congress on the subject of
      <a href="/wiki/Nanotechnology" title="Nanotechnology">
       nanotechnology
      </a>
      , advocating that nanotechnology has the potential to solve serious global problems such as poverty, disease, and climate change. "Nanotech Could Give Global Warming a Big Chill".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_warming_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Global_warming-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In media appearances, Kurzweil has stressed the extreme potential dangers of nanotechnology
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-booktv_18-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-booktv-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but argues that in practice, progress cannot be stopped because that would require a totalitarian system, and any attempt to do so would drive dangerous technologies underground and deprive responsible scientists of the tools needed for defense. He suggests that the proper place of regulation is to ensure that technological progress proceeds safely and quickly, but does not deprive the world of profound benefits. He stated, "To avoid dangers such as unrestrained nanobot replication, we need relinquishment at the right level and to place our highest priority on the continuing advance of defensive technologies, staying ahead of destructive technologies. An overall strategy should include a streamlined regulatory process, a global program of monitoring for unknown or evolving biological pathogens, temporary moratoriums, raising public awareness, international cooperation, software reconnaissance, and fostering values of liberty, tolerance, and respect for knowledge and diversity."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Health_and_aging">
       Health and aging
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ray_Kurzweil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Health and aging">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Kurzweil admits that he cared little for his health until age 35, when he was found to suffer from a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glucose_intolerance" title="Glucose intolerance">
       glucose intolerance
      </a>
      , an early form of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_II_diabetes" title="Type II diabetes">
       type II diabetes
      </a>
      (a major risk factor for
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_disease" title="Heart disease">
       heart disease
      </a>
      ). Kurzweil then found a doctor (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terry_Grossman" title="Terry Grossman">
       Terry Grossman
      </a>
      , M.D.) who shares his non-conventional beliefs to develop an extreme regimen involving hundreds of pills,
      <a href="/wiki/Intravenous_therapy" title="Intravenous therapy">
       chemical intravenous
      </a>
      treatments,
      <a href="/wiki/Red_wine" title="Red wine">
       red wine
      </a>
      and various other methods to attempt to live longer. Kurzweil was ingesting "250 supplements, eight to 10 glasses of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkaline_water" title="Alkaline water">
       alkaline water
      </a>
      and 10 cups of
      <a href="/wiki/Green_tea" title="Green tea">
       green tea
      </a>
      " every day and drinking several glasses of red wine a week in an effort to "reprogram" his biochemistry.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Lately, he has cut down the number of supplement pills to 150.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Kurzweil joined the
      <a href="/wiki/Alcor_Life_Extension_Foundation" title="Alcor Life Extension Foundation">
       Alcor Life Extension Foundation
      </a>
      , a
      <a href="/wiki/Cryonics" title="Cryonics">
       cryonics
      </a>
      company. In the event of his declared death, Kurzweil will be perfused with
      <a href="/wiki/Cryoprotectant" title="Cryoprotectant">
       cryoprotectants
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cryopreservation#Vitrification" title="Cryopreservation">
       vitrified
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Liquid_nitrogen" title="Liquid nitrogen">
       liquid nitrogen
      </a>
      , and stored at an Alcor facility in the hope that future medical technology will be able to repair his tissues and revive him.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      He has authored three books on the subjects of nutrition, health and immortality:
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_10%25_Solution_for_a_Healthy_Life" title="The 10% Solution for a Healthy Life">
        The 10% Solution for a Healthy Life
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Fantastic_Voyage:_Live_Long_Enough_to_Live_Forever" title="Fantastic Voyage: Live Long Enough to Live Forever">
        Fantastic Voyage: Live Long Enough to Live Forever
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Transcend: Nine Steps to Living Well Forever
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In all, he recommends that other people emulate his health practices to the best of their abilities. Kurzweil and his current "anti-aging" doctor,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terry_Grossman" title="Terry Grossman">
       Terry Grossman
      </a>
      , MD., now have two websites promoting their first
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and second book.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      He asserts that in the future, everyone will live forever.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In a 2013 interview, Kurzweil said that in 15 years, medical technology could add more than a year to one's remaining life expectancy for each year that passes, and we could then "outrun our own deaths". He has been an extreme advocate of
      <a href="/wiki/SENS_Research_Foundation" title="SENS Research Foundation">
       SENS Research Foundation
      </a>
      for the successful defeating of aging, and has encouraged acts of donation to hasten their rejuvenation research.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wsj_28-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-wsj-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Kurzweil.27s_view_of_the_human_neocortex">
       Kurzweil's view of the human neocortex
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ray_Kurzweil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Kurzweil's view of the human neocortex">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      According to Kurzweil, technologists will be creating synthetic neocortexes based on the operating principles of the human
      <a href="/wiki/Neocortex" title="Neocortex">
       neocortex
      </a>
      with the primary purpose of extending our own neocortexes. He claims to believe that the neocortex of an adult human consists of approximately 300 million pattern recognizers. He draws on the commonly accepted belief that the primary anatomical difference between humans and other primates that allowed for superior intellectual abilities was the evolution of a larger neocortex. He claims that the six-layered neocortex deals with increasing abstraction from one layer to the next. He says that at the low levels, the neocortex may seem cold and mechanical because it can only make simple decisions, but at the higher levels of the hierarchy, the neocortex is likely to be dealing with concepts like being funny, being sexy, expressing a loving sentiment, creating a poem or understanding a poem, etc. He claims to believe that these higher levels of the human neocortex were the enabling factors to permit the human development of language, technology, art, and science. He stated, "If the quantitative improvement from primates to humans with the big forehead was the enabling factor to allow for language, technology, art, and science, what kind of qualitative leap can we make with another quantitative increase? Why not go from 300 million pattern recognizers to a billion?
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Encouraging_futurism_and_transhumanism">
       Encouraging futurism and transhumanism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ray_Kurzweil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Encouraging futurism and transhumanism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Kurzweil's standing as a
      <a href="/wiki/Futures_studies" title="Futures studies">
       futurist
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Transhumanism" title="Transhumanism">
       transhumanist
      </a>
      has led to his involvement in several singularity-themed organizations. In December 2004, Kurzweil joined the advisory board of the
      <a href="/wiki/Machine_Intelligence_Research_Institute" title="Machine Intelligence Research Institute">
       Machine Intelligence Research Institute
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In October 2005, Kurzweil joined the scientific advisory board of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lifeboat_Foundation" title="Lifeboat Foundation">
       Lifeboat Foundation
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On May 13, 2006, Kurzweil was the first speaker at the
      <a href="/wiki/Singularity_Summit" title="Singularity Summit">
       Singularity Summit
      </a>
      at
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stanford" title="Stanford">
       Stanford
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In May 2013, Kurzweil was the keynote speaker at the 2013 proceeding of the Research, Innovation, Start-up and Employment (RISE) international conference in
      <a href="/wiki/Seoul" title="Seoul">
       Seoul
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">
       Korea Republic
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In February 2009, Kurzweil, in collaboration with
      <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">
       Google
      </a>
      and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NASA_Ames_Research_Center" title="NASA Ames Research Center">
       NASA Ames Research Center
      </a>
      , announced the creation of the
      <a href="/wiki/Singularity_University" title="Singularity University">
       Singularity University
      </a>
      training center for corporate executives and government officials. The University's self-described mission is to "assemble, educate and inspire a cadre of leaders who strive to understand and facilitate the development of exponentially advancing technologies and apply, focus and guide these tools to address humanity's grand challenges".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Using
      <a href="/wiki/Vernor_Vinge" title="Vernor Vinge">
       Vernor Vinge
      </a>
      's Singularity concept as a foundation, the university offered its first nine-week graduate program to 40 students in June, 2009.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Predictions">
       Predictions
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ray_Kurzweil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Predictions">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Predictions_made_by_Ray_Kurzweil" title="Predictions made by Ray Kurzweil">
       Predictions made by Ray Kurzweil
      </a>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Past_predictions">
       Past predictions
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ray_Kurzweil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Past predictions">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Kurzweil's first book,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Age_of_Intelligent_Machines" title="The Age of Intelligent Machines">
        The Age of Intelligent Machines
       </a>
      </i>
      , presented his ideas about the future. It was written from 1986 to 1989 and published in 1990. Building on
      <a href="/wiki/Ithiel_de_Sola_Pool" title="Ithiel de Sola Pool">
       Ithiel de Sola Pool
      </a>
      's "Technologies of Freedom" (1983), Kurzweil claims to have forecast the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Soviet_Union_(1985%E2%80%931991)" title="History of the Soviet Union (19851991)">
       demise of the Soviet Union
      </a>
      due to new technologies such as cellular phones and fax machines disempowering authoritarian governments by removing state control over the flow of information.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the book Kurzweil also extrapolated preexisting trends in the improvement of computer chess software performance to predict that computers would beat the best human players "by the year 2000".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In May 1997 chess World Champion
      <a href="/wiki/Garry_Kasparov" title="Garry Kasparov">
       Garry Kasparov
      </a>
      was defeated by IBM's
      <a href="/wiki/Deep_Blue_(chess_computer)" title="Deep Blue (chess computer)">
       Deep Blue
      </a>
      computer in a well-publicized chess tournament.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Perhaps most significantly, Kurzweil foresaw the explosive growth in worldwide Internet use that began in the 1990s. At the time of the publication of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Age_of_Intelligent_Machines" title="The Age of Intelligent Machines">
        The Age of Intelligent Machines
       </a>
      </i>
      , there were only 2.6 million Internet users in the world,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the medium was unreliable, difficult to use, and deficient in content. He also stated that the Internet would explode not only in the number of users but in content as well, eventually granting users access "to international networks of libraries, data bases, and information services". Additionally, Kurzweil claims to have correctly foreseen that the preferred mode of Internet access would inevitably be through wireless systems, and he was also correct to estimate that the latter would become practical for widespread use in the early 21st century.
     </p>
     <p>
      Kurzweil's predictions for 2009 were mostly inaccurate, claims
      <i>
       Forbes
      </i>
      magazine. For example, Kurzweil predicted, "The majority of text is created using continuous speech recognition." This is not the case.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Future_predictions">
       Future predictions
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ray_Kurzweil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Future predictions">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In 1999, Kurzweil published a second book titled
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Age_of_Spiritual_Machines" title="The Age of Spiritual Machines">
        The Age of Spiritual Machines
       </a>
      </i>
      , which goes into more depth explaining his futurist ideas. The third and final part of the book is devoted to predictions over the coming century, from 2009 through 2099. In
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Singularity_Is_Near" title="The Singularity Is Near">
        The Singularity Is Near
       </a>
      </i>
      he makes fewer concrete short-term predictions, but includes many longer-term visions.
     </p>
     <p>
      He states that with radical life extension will come radical life enhancement. He says he is confident that within 10 years we will have the option to spend some of our time in 3D virtual environments that appear just as real as real reality, but these will not yet be made possible via direct interaction with our nervous system. "If you look at video games and how we went from pong to the virtual reality we have available today, it is highly likely that immortality in essence will be possible." He claims to know that 20 to 25 years from now, we will have millions of blood-cell sized devices, known as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanobots" title="Nanobots">
       nanobots
      </a>
      , inside our bodies fighting against diseases, improving our memory, and cognitive abilities. Kurzweil claims to know that a machine will pass the
      <a href="/wiki/Turing_test" title="Turing test">
       Turing test
      </a>
      by 2029, and that around 2045, "the pace of change will be so astonishingly quick that we won't be able to keep up, unless we enhance our own intelligence by merging with the intelligent machines we are creating". Shortly after, Kurzweil claims to know that humans will be a hybrid of biological and non-biological intelligence that becomes increasingly dominated by its non-biological component. He stresses that "AI is not an intelligent invasion from Mars. These are brain extenders that we have created to expand our own mental reach. They are part of our civilization. They are part of who we are. So over the next few decades our human-machine civilization will become increasingly dominated by its non-biological component. In
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Transcendent_Man" title="Transcendent Man">
        Transcendent Man
       </a>
      </i>
      Kurzweil states "We humans are going to start linking with each other and become a metaconnection we will all be connected and all be omni-present, plugged into this global network that is connected to billions of people, and filled with data."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Kurzweil states in a press conference that we are the only species that goes beyond our limitations- "we didn't stay in the caves, we didn't stay on the planet, and we're not going to stay with the limitations of our biology". In his singularity based documentary he is quoted saying "I think people are fooling themselves when they say they have accepted death".
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2008, Kurzweil said in an expert panel in the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Academy_of_Engineering" title="National Academy of Engineering">
       National Academy of Engineering
      </a>
      that
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">
       solar power
      </a>
      will scale up to produce all the energy needs of Earth's people in 20 years. According to Kurzweil, we only need to capture 1 part in 10,000 of the energy from the Sun that hits Earth's surface to meet all of humanity's energy needs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">
       Reception
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ray_Kurzweil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Reception">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Praise">
       Praise
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ray_Kurzweil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Praise">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Kurzweil was referred to as "the ultimate thinking machine" by
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">
        Forbes
       </a>
      </i>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pfeiffer_8-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-pfeiffer-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and as a "restless genius"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bulkeley_A3A_7-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Bulkeley_A3A-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      by
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">
        The Wall Street Journal
       </a>
      </i>
      . PBS included Kurzweil as one of 16 "revolutionaries who made America"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Who_Made_America_9-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Who_Made_America-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      along with other inventors of the past two centuries.
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Inc._(magazine)" title="Inc. (magazine)">
        Inc.
       </a>
      </i>
      magazine ranked him #8 among the "most fascinating" entrepreneurs in the United States and called him "Edison's rightful heir".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26_Most_Fascinating_Entrepreneurs_10-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-26_Most_Fascinating_Entrepreneurs-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Kurzweil has received many awards and honors, including:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       First place in the 1965 International Science Fair
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ssp_19-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ssp-19">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         19
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       for inventing the classical music synthesizing computer.
      </li>
      <li>
       The 1978
       <a href="/wiki/Grace_Murray_Hopper_Award" title="Grace Murray Hopper Award">
        Grace Murray Hopper Award
       </a>
       from the
       <a href="/wiki/Association_for_Computing_Machinery" title="Association for Computing Machinery">
        Association for Computing Machinery
       </a>
       . The award is given annually to one "outstanding young computer professional" and is accompanied by a $35,000 prize.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         70
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Kurzweil won it for his invention of the Kurzweil Reading Machine.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         71
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       In 1986, Kurzweil was named Honorary Chairman for Innovation of the White House Conference on Small Business by President Reagan
      </li>
      <li>
       In 1988, Kurzweil was named Inventor of the Year by MIT and the Boston Museum of Science.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kurzweilai.net_72-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-kurzweilai.net-72">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         72
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       In 1990, Kurzweil was voted Engineer of the Year by the over one million readers of Design News Magazine and received their third annual Technology Achievement Award
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kurzweilai.net_72-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-kurzweilai.net-72">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         72
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         73
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       The 1994 Dickson Prize in Science. One is awarded every year by
       <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_University" title="Carnegie Mellon University">
        Carnegie Mellon University
       </a>
       to individuals who have "notably advanced the field of science." Both a medal and a $50,000 prize are presented to winners.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         74
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       The 1998 "Inventor of the Year" award from the
       <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">
        Massachusetts Institute of Technology
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         75
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       The 1999
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Medal_of_Technology" title="National Medal of Technology">
        National Medal of Technology
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         76
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       This is the highest award the President of the United States can bestow upon individuals and groups for pioneering new technologies, and the President dispenses the award at his discretion.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         <span>
          [
